About 45 years ago I was posted to sort out a failing branch.  I walked into the office to find staff smoking, loafing about, wearing jeans, T shirts and trainers.  The place was a mess and the manager was not in control.  I asked them to stop smoking, start working and return after lunch (they went home for lunch) in office clothing of trousers, shirts and ties (for men) and skirts/blouses etc (for women).  Remember 45 years ago.  They came back exactly the same and I sacked them on the spot.   Unions were involved but they'd all broken their contracts and the firm stood by me. The manager and his wife couldn't hack it & left too. They would have all gone anyway as they weren't making a loss and the branch would have closed.  I felt bad, but I single handed turned round that office by sheer hard work and got it up running to employ new management and staff who were glad of a job and proud of what they did.  I moved onto the next one before my 21st birthday.  Some years later I had my own company and an interviewee came in 20 minutes late carrying her food shopping and dressed all in black (Goth).  She asked me to shut any windows and doors as she was scared of bee stings.  Unfortunately she didn't get the job.   Never fails to amaze me.

Good one. I first finished an apprenticeship in metal working and a few years of installing industrial machines before I figured I don't want to have the grease and dirt eaten into my skin for life. I own a small business.  We schedule roughly 40-ish interviews a week.  Of those, maybe 10 will actually even show up.  Of those, maybe 4 of them will actually be on time.  Of the ones on time, we will hire about 2 (obviously implying we don't hire the ones not on time).  Of the two we hire, only one will last longer than 3 weeks.  So yes, of those 40 interviews scheduled, we will get about 1 hire that actually lasts and is decent at the job.  Generally younger people apply for this job (those in their 20's and 30's).  It's not just people working at Twitter or other tech companies that are like this actress, it truly is just young people in general these days.  Work ethic is essentially non-existant in you younger generations (millenial/gen Zs).  We see eerily similar entitlement to this actress in so many of the candidates we interview, particularly the ones that are late.  Not to say there aren't good, hard working young people out there, but from personal experience I can tell you it is most definitely the exception, and not the norm.  If I didn't see this for myself day in and day out I would say it's just some blanket generalization that younger people these days are lazy, but I do see it and it's not a stereotype - it's the absolute truth.

So, I'm still for agile work, similarly to how you don't expect a Stationary Engineer or similar titles to do 9-5, some tech titles shouldn't be using that same model.  I'll illustrate: A programmer who is very good at their job will over a few months learn best practices and get their daily/weekly assignments done in 2 hours. Other members of that team will be doing their thing once he/she finishes their part and for the most part except maybe Q/A nothing else needs them to be there.  Not all jobs for programmers are like this, some enterprise jobs use programmers as a kind of IT which IMO they are way out of title for but w/e. An IT guy makes about 45 - 70k a programmer can make 50-200k a year. For the purposes of this I'm only really talking about entry level so 45 and 55k respectively.   I get the jealousy factor but it comes off as other people setting the norms on what should be a decision made on the facts relevant to the employee and employer.   If you have someone who can finish a weeks work in 2-4 hours at 55k rather than 200k. I'd rather that guy than someone who  would take 40 hours to do the same work. My other teams would be ahead of the curve by 36 hours. In that case I'd just expect him/her to be available within working hours for Q/A.  To go back to the blue collar example in the open; Forcing an SE to work 9-5 may work for some places but for others that's a 24 hour position. It's supposed to be always active, so scheduling some one 7 days a week at the exact time is ignoring the demands of the job because the loudest morons out there are sheeping to this 9-5 nonsense.   Finally, I am certain there are folks who just don't work, I'm certain because I saw it all the time when I used to do 9-5. People who pretend to work in their office but are just shuffling papers and talking on their ear piece. Then when reports aren't done they blame someone from another department. I've also seen the opposite during COVID where people who were getting work done were getting more done and were happy af when I did see them at the job site.  I remember one guy actually said he could afford to pay a cab to come to work to avoid 3 hour transit for things he needed to be there for each week. The trip was 15-20 minutes by cab and he would show up to work at like 5am knock out his tasks, when the VP showed up he touched bases with everyone and then left back and was producing his reports from home. He told me the best part was not having to deal with office drama and just focusing on the work.

Best perk.  I worked for the DOD.  We built fully functional flight simulators of the AH-1 Cobra helicopter.  The team took actual Cobra helicopters and turned them into flight simulators.  They were very realistic.  Full visuals, audio, seat shakers, helmet tracking etc.  The instructor could enter failures and the appropriate circuit breaker would pop and the "software" would introduce the failure.
